      <p>
       The
       <b>
        Exploratorium
       </b>
       is a museum in
       <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">
        San Francisco
       </a>
       that allows visitors to explore the world through science, art, and human perception. Its mission is to create inquiry-based experiences that transform learning worldwide. It has been described by the
       <i>
        New York Times
       </i>
       as the most important science museum to have opened since the mid-20th century, an achievement attributed to "the nature of its exhibits, its wide-ranging influence and its sophisticated teacher training program".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawlessness_3-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lawlessness-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Characterized as "a mad scientist's penny arcade, a scientific funhouse, and an experimental laboratory all rolled into one",
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCarthy_4-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-McCarthy-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the participatory nature of its exhibits and its self-identification as a center for informal learning has led to it being cited as the prototype for participatory museums around the world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thrill_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Thrill-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Exploratorium was founded by physicist and educator
       <a href="/wiki/Frank_Oppenheimer" title="Frank Oppenheimer">
        Frank Oppenheimer
       </a>
       and opened in 1969 at the
       <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Fine_Arts" title="Palace of Fine Arts">
        Palace of Fine Arts
       </a>
       , its home until January 2, 2013. On April 17, 2013, the Exploratorium reopened at Piers 15 and 17 on San Francisco's
       <a href="/wiki/Embarcadero_(San_Francisco)" title="Embarcadero (San Francisco)">
        Embarcadero
       </a>
       . The historic interior and exterior of Pier 15 was renovated extensively prior to the move, and is divided into several galleries mainly separated by content, including the physics of seeing and listening (Light and Sound), Human Behavior, Living Systems, Tinkering (including electricity and magnetism), the Outdoor Gallery, and the Bay Observatory Gallery, which focuses on local environment, weather, and landscape.
      </p>
      <p>
       Since the museum's founding, over 1,000 participatory exhibits have been created, approximately 600 of which are on the floor at any given time. The exhibit-building workshop space is contained within the museum and is open to view. In addition to the public exhibition space, the Exploratorium has been engaged in the professional development of teachers, science education reform, and the promotion of museums as informal education centers since its founding. Since Oppenheimer's death in 1985, the Exploratorium has expanded into other domains, including its 50,000-page website and two iPad apps on sound and color. It has also inspired an international network of participatory museums working to engage the public with general science education.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chang_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chang-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The new Exploratorium building is also working to showcase environmental sustainability efforts as part of its goal to become the largest net-zero museum in the country.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SFGateNetZero_7-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SFGateNetZero-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Exploratorium offers visitors a variety of ways—including exhibits, webcasts, websites and events—to explore and understand the world around them. In 2011, the Exploratorium received the
       <a href="/wiki/National_Science_Board" title="National Science Board">
        National Science Board
       </a>
       2011 Public Service Science Award for its contributions to public understanding of science and engineering.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSF2011_8-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NSF2011-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Exploratorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inside_exploratorium.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="425" data-file-width="640" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Inside_exploratorium.jpg/220px-Inside_exploratorium.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Inside_exploratorium.jpg/330px-Inside_exploratorium.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Inside_exploratorium.jpg/440px-Inside_exploratorium.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inside_exploratorium.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Exploratorium's main floor, in its original
         <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Fine_Arts" title="Palace of Fine Arts">
          Palace of Fine Arts
         </a>
         location (2009)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Founding_and_early_years">
        Founding and early years
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Exploratorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Founding and early years">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frank_Oppenheimer.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="301" height="361" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Frank_Oppenheimer.jpg/170px-Frank_Oppenheimer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Frank_Oppenheimer.jpg/255px-Frank_Oppenheimer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fd/Frank_Oppenheimer.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Frank_Oppenheimer.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Frank_Oppenheimer" title="Frank Oppenheimer">
          Frank Oppenheimer
         </a>
         , founder of the Exploratorium
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Exploratorium is the brainchild of
       <a href="/wiki/Frank_Oppenheimer" title="Frank Oppenheimer">
        Frank Oppenheimer
       </a>
       , an experimental physicist and university professor. Oppenheimer, who worked on the
       <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">
        Manhattan Project
       </a>
       with his brother
       <a href="/wiki/J._Robert_Oppenheimer" title="J. Robert Oppenheimer">
        J. Robert Oppenheimer
       </a>
       , was diverted from an academic career when he was forced to resign from his position at the
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_Minnesota" title="University of Minnesota">
        University of Minnesota
       </a>
       in 1949 as a result of an inquiry by the
       <a href="/wiki/House_Un-American_Activities_Committee" title="House Un-American Activities Committee">
        House Un-American Activities Committee
       </a>
       . He was blacklisted from academic positions across the country, and withdrew with his family to run a Colorado
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cattle_ranch" title="Cattle ranch">
        cattle ranch
       </a>
       for almost a decade.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cole_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cole-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         104–115
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Oppenheimer also began lending a hand with science projects of local high school students, eventually becoming the sole science teacher at the high school in
       <a href="/wiki/Pagosa_Springs,_Colorado" title="Pagosa Springs, Colorado">
        Pagosa Springs, Colorado
       </a>
       . The field trips and experiments he did with his high school students would become a blueprint for the hands-on methods of teaching and learning he would later bring to the Exploratorium.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cole_9-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cole-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         115–121
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       When Oppenheimer was invited to join the University of Colorado's physics department in 1959, he found himself less interested in traditional laboratory research and much more interested in exploring methods of provoking curiosity and inquiry. He received a grant from the National Science Foundation, which he used to build models of nearly a hundred science experiments.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cole_9-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cole-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         136–137
        </span>
       </sup>
       This "Library of Experiments" would become the core of the Exploratorium exhibit collection, and was the forerunner of the
       <i>
        Exploratorium Cookbook
       </i>
       , a manual explaining how to build these basic science exhibits.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cookbook_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cookbook-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Convinced of the need for public museums to supplement science curricula at all levels, he toured Europe and studied museums on a
       <a href="/wiki/Guggenheim_Fellowship" title="Guggenheim Fellowship">
        Guggenheim Fellowship
       </a>
       in 1965. Three European museums, encountered during that year, served as important influences on the founding of the Exploratorium: the
       <a href="/wiki/Palais_de_la_D%C3%A9couverte" title="Palais de la Découverte">
        Palais de la Découverte
       </a>
       , which displayed models to teach scientific concepts and employed students as demonstrators, a practice that directly inspired the Exploratorium's much-lauded High School Explainer Program; the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Kensington_Museum" title="South Kensington Museum">
        South Kensington Museum
       </a>
       of Science and Art, which Oppenheimer and his wife visited frequently; and the
       <a href="/wiki/Deutsches_Museum" title="Deutsches Museum">
        Deutsches Museum
       </a>
       in Munich, the world's largest science museum, which had a number of interactive displays that impressed the Oppenheimers.
      </p>
      <p>
       Back in the United States, Oppenheimer was invited to do the initial planning for a new branch of the
       <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">
        Smithsonian
       </a>
       , but he turned it down to work on what he called his "San Francisco project". In 1967, the Oppenheimers came to
       <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">
        San Francisco
       </a>
       with a view towards opening an independent museum of their own. Oppenheimer sought funding and support for the endeavor using a grassroots approach, bringing a written proposal and some handmade exhibits with him as he visited scientists, businesses, city and school officials, relatives, and friends. Many prominent scientists and cultural figures endorsed the project, and the offers of support in conjunction with a $50,000 grant from the
       <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Foundation" title="San Francisco Foundation">
        San Francisco Foundation
       </a>
       made the museum realizable.
      </p>
      <p>
       In late August 1969, the Exploratorium opened with little fanfare at the Palace of Fine Arts. Oppenheimer "simply opened the doors".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cole_9-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cole-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         152
        </span>
       </sup>
       Although the building needed many improvements, Oppenheimer couldn't afford to make the changes, and decided to allow the public to come and watch exhibits being built and changes being made as part of the participatory ethos of the institution.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cole_9-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cole-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         128–152
        </span>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MuseAsLab_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MuseAsLab-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       An early proposal would have erected a wall between the workshop where exhibits were being developed and the main visitor areas. Instead, Oppenheimer insisted that the workshop be placed without a wall, right next to the main entrance so that visitors could experience "the way a shop smells when you burn the wood in a saw, or smell the oil from a lathe".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cole_9-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cole-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         156
        </span>
       </sup>
       Above the workshop was a sign, made by the wife of
       <a href="/wiki/George_Gamow" title="George Gamow">
        George Gamow
       </a>
       , a physicist, educator, and friend of Oppenheimer who had died just a year before the opening of the Exploratorium.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cole_9-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cole-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         152,157
        </span>
       </sup>
       The inscription said "Here Is Being Created the Exploratorium a Community Museum Dedicated to Awareness".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cole_9-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cole-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         157
        </span>
       </sup>
       (Today, a copy of this motto is inscribed above the main entrance to the new Exploratorium at Pier 15.)
      </p>
      <p>
       Oppenheimer served as the museum’s director until his death in 1985. Dr. Robert L. White served as Director from 1987 to 1990. Dr.
       <a href="/wiki/Go%C3%A9ry_Delac%C3%B4te" title="Goéry Delacôte">
        Goéry Delacôte
       </a>
       served as Executive Director from 1991 until 2005.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-businessweek_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-businessweek-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Dr. Dennis Bartels served as Executive Director of the Exploratorium from 2006 to 2016. In June 2016, the Exploratorium welcomed its newest director, Chris Flink.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The museum has expanded greatly since the 1980s, increasing outreach, expanding programs for educators, creating an expanded Web presence, and forming museum partnerships around the world.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Move_to_Piers_15_and_17">
        Move to Piers 15 and 17
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Exploratorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Move to Piers 15 and 17">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The Exploratorium relocated from the Palace of Fine Arts to Piers 15 and 17, located between the
       <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Ferry_Building" title="San Francisco Ferry Building">
        San Francisco Ferry Building
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Pier_39" title="Pier 39">
        Pier 39
       </a>
       along the San Francisco Embarcadero, in April 2013. The Piers location was identified by Goéry Delacôte and then-board chairman Van Kasper as a potential space for relocation in 2004.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Build_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Build-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         11
        </span>
       </sup>
       In 2005, the San Francisco Board of Supervisors passed a resolution exempting the museum’s 66-year lease of the piers from San Francisco’s competitive bidding process due to its unique nature as a cultural and educational institution.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sadiq_15-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sadiq-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Groundbreaking for the project, which required substantial construction and renovation, occurred on October 19, 2010. The Exploratorium holds a 66-year lease on the piers with the Port of San Francisco. Exhibits are currently only viewable at the Pier 15 campus; Pier 17 houses some staff, with the option for future expansion.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perlman2010_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Perlman2010-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Piers 15 and 17 are historic piers, built in 1931 and 1912 respectively.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Under_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Under-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         21
        </span>
       </sup>
       In 1954, the area between the piers was infilled and paved over. This infill was removed as part of the construction phase, restoring the space between the piers to public plazas, a pedestrian bridge, and open water.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture_and_design">
        Architecture and design
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Exploratorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Architecture and design">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Renovation_of_Piers_15_and_17">
        Renovation of Piers 15 and 17
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Exploratorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Renovation of Piers 15 and 17">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rust_wedge.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="1840" height="302" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Rust_wedge.jpg/170px-Rust_wedge.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Rust_wedge.jpg/255px-Rust_wedge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Rust_wedge.jpg/340px-Rust_wedge.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rust_wedge.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Outdoors "Rust Wedge" display shows the enormous expansive force of
         <a href="/wiki/Rust" title="Rust">
          rusting iron
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Exploratorium campus comprises 330,000 sq ft (31,000 m
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       ) of indoor and outdoor exhibit space, and includes 1.5 acres (0.61 ha) of freely accessible public space. The exhibits are housed in and around Pier 15, which extends over 800 ft (240 m) over the Bay.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MovingScience_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MovingScience-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Exploratorium at Pier 15 was designed by architecture firm
       <a href="/wiki/EHDD" title="EHDD">
        EHDD
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Breakthrough_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Breakthrough-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Nibbi Brothers served as the General Contractor,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Exploratorium,_Designed_by_EHDD_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Exploratorium,_Designed_by_EHDD-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AGA_(Architectural_Glass_and_Aluminum)" title="AGA (Architectural Glass and Aluminum)">
        AGA (Architectural Glass and Aluminum)
       </a>
       as the Glazing Contractor. The piers had been neglected for decades leading up to the Exploratorium’s move, and extensive renovation and repair was required.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Build_14-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Build-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         11
        </span>
       </sup>
       Nearly two thirds of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Piling" title="Piling">
        pilings
       </a>
       under Pier 15 were repaired, including almost every piling needed to provide structural integrity, and new pilings were sunk.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Build_14-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Build-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         24
        </span>
       </sup>
       The removal of the parking lot between the piers was done slowly over the two years of construction, and the debris from the removal was captured and recycled.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Build_14-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Build-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         67
        </span>
       </sup>
       Several pilings were left in the water between the piers, both for aesthetic reasons and to support future exhibits.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Build_14-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Build-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         47
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       An effort was made in the construction of the new location to preserve the historic elements of Pier 15. The Bay Observatory was the only new structure added to the site. The east end of the pier was cleaned of
       <a href="/wiki/Lead_paint" title="Lead paint">
        lead paint
       </a>
       , revealing historic lettering underneath; designers chose to preserve the lettering rather than paint it over.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Build_14-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Build-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         67
        </span>
       </sup>
       As a result, the traces of the shipping lines that originally frequented the pier can still be seen. Some of the preservation efforts presented challenges in design, however; historic windows created energy losses that had to be offset elsewhere, and the historic interior trusswork was mainly restored rather than removed, meaning that the upper-level staff offices had to be built around them.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sustain_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sustain-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         30
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Other challenges to the design of the facilities were presented by the museum’s sustainability initiatives. The use of natural light whenever possible challenged exhibit designers relying on carefully controlled light levels; this was solved by using curtains and glare-reducing paint colors.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sustain_21-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sustain-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         32
        </span>
       </sup>
       Other conflicts between construction and energy use included the glass in the Observatory, which would have presented a problem in cooling the building on warm days. This was overcome by adding fritted glass to the windows in thin horizontal lines through the panes to decrease the transparency without affecting the views.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sustain_21-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sustain-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         85
        </span>
       </sup>
       The fritting also makes the reflective surfaces of the Bay Observatory safe for birds.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TakingFlight_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-TakingFlight-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Pier 15 incorporated two seismic joints as part of its
       <a href="/wiki/Seismic_retrofit" title="Seismic retrofit">
        seismic retrofit
       </a>
       , one separating the Bay Observatory from the Pier 15 shed and the other separating the entire pier from the land. This second joint ensures that the entire pier will move independently from the land mass in the event of an earthquake, significantly reducing the potential
       <a href="/wiki/Torsion_(mechanics)" title="Torsion (mechanics)">
        torsional
       </a>
       stress.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Build_14-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Build-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         83
        </span>
       </sup>
       The café at the west end of the Exploratorium is named the Seismic Joint in honor of the joint, which cuts through the area of the building where the café is situated.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Build_14-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-Build-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         80
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The aesthetic of the project was defined as "industrial naval chic" in keeping with the pier’s history.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Build_14-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-Build-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         63
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Restaurant">
        Restaurant
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Exploratorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Restaurant">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Seaglass_Restaurant_at_the_Exploratorium.jpeg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="853" data-file-width="1280" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Seaglass_Restaurant_at_the_Exploratorium.jpeg/220px-Seaglass_Restaurant_at_the_Exploratorium.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Seaglass_Restaurant_at_the_Exploratorium.jpeg/330px-Seaglass_Restaurant_at_the_Exploratorium.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Seaglass_Restaurant_at_the_Exploratorium.jpeg/440px-Seaglass_Restaurant_at_the_Exploratorium.jpeg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Seaglass_Restaurant_at_the_Exploratorium.jpeg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The design scheme for the Exploratorium's Seaglass Restaurant was inspired by the exhibit
         <i>
          Color of Water
         </i>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The design aesthetic for both the Seismic Joint and Seaglass Restaurant was created by designer Olle Lundberg and based on the exhibit
       <i>
        Color of Water
       </i>
       . The bar at Seaglass features a specially designed version of Exploratorium artist Shawn Lani’s exhibit
       <i>
        Icy Bodies
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feasters_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Feasters-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LorettaKeller_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-LorettaKeller-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sustainability">
        Sustainability
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Exploratorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Sustainability">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The Exploratorium at Pier 15 has a net-zero energy goal as part of its overall sustainability efforts. Setting this net-zero goal means that, while in operation, The Exploratorium will produce more energy on-site than it will consume on an annual basis.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The museum highlights its sustainability efforts in visible ways throughout the museum as part of a stated intention to lead by example.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TruongEcoFriendly_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-TruongEcoFriendly-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Exploratorium, in order to reach its net-zero energy goal, produces energy with a roof-mounted array of
       <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaics" title="Photovoltaics">
        photovoltaics
       </a>
       . There are 5,874 PV modules on the roof, totaling 78,712 square feet (7,312.6 m
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       ), with a projected year-1 yield of 1.3 MW-AC/square foot (13.9 MW-AC/m
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       ), or a total year-1 yield of 2,113,715 kWh.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sustain_21-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sustain-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ehdd.com_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ehdd.com-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Any surplus energy generated is intended to be fed back into the utility grid, as the projected annual energy use for the building totals at 1,275,936 kWh.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abellon_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Abellon-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Schematic_diagram_of_bay_water_heating_and_cooling_system_at_The_Exploratorium,_San_Francisco.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="267" data-file-width="373" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d8/Schematic_diagram_of_bay_water_heating_and_cooling_system_at_The_Exploratorium%2C_San_Francisco.png/220px-Schematic_diagram_of_bay_water_heating_and_cooling_system_at_The_Exploratorium%2C_San_Francisco.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d8/Schematic_diagram_of_bay_water_heating_and_cooling_system_at_The_Exploratorium%2C_San_Francisco.png/330px-Schematic_diagram_of_bay_water_heating_and_cooling_system_at_The_Exploratorium%2C_San_Francisco.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/d8/Schematic_diagram_of_bay_water_heating_and_cooling_system_at_The_Exploratorium%2C_San_Francisco.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Schematic_diagram_of_bay_water_heating_and_cooling_system_at_The_Exploratorium,_San_Francisco.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Diagram of bay water heating and cooling system at The Exploratorium
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In addition to solar power, the museum makes use of an
       <a href="/wiki/HVAC" title="HVAC">
        HVAC
       </a>
       system that takes advantage of the relatively constant, moderate temperature of the bay water under the piers, which is 50 to 65 °F (10 to 18 °C), to heat and cool the building. The bay water is filtered and sterilized before it is brought into a 4,000 US gallons (15 m
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       ) cistern below the pier, where it is held for use. When needed, the bay water is moved to a titanium
       <a href="/wiki/Heat_exchanger" title="Heat exchanger">
        heat exchanger
       </a>
       , of which the building has two, where it is either used to heat or cool water that is cycled through a system of thermally activated radiant slabs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Exploring_Every_Possibility_31-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Exploring_Every_Possibility-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There are 27 miles (43 km) of plastic tubing in the
       <a href="/wiki/Radiant_heating" title="Radiant heating">
        radiant heating
       </a>
       system in the floor, creating 82 different heating and cooling zones with distinct control systems.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Build_14-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-Build-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       After the bay water passes through the heat exchangers, it is returned to the San Francisco Bay as allowed by a permit issued by the California Regional Water Quality Control Board.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Most of the energy savings are expected to come from using the bay water as a
       <a href="/wiki/Heat_sink" title="Heat sink">
        heat sink
       </a>
       for the building’s cooling needs. When the temperature of the bay water is below that of the chilled water return from the radiant pipes, which is the case for most of the year in the Bay Area’s temperate climate, the system works in waterside
       <a href="/wiki/Economizer" title="Economizer">
        economizer
       </a>
       mode. In this mode, the cooling loads are met either entirely or partially through passive heat exchange between the colder bay water and the warmer return water, greatly reducing the building’s energy needs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abellon_30-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Abellon-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Exploratorium at Pier 15 has a separate system for its ventilation needs, pairing a
       <a href="/wiki/Dedicated_outdoor_air_system" title="Dedicated outdoor air system">
        dedicated outdoor air system
       </a>
       (DOAS) with
       <a href="/wiki/Displacement_ventilation" title="Displacement ventilation">
        displacement ventilation
       </a>
       distribution to bring outdoor air into the building. By integrating radiant heating and cooling and displacement ventilation, the Exploratorium has greatly reduced the portion of its HVAC system that relies on forced air. Reducing the size of a building’s forced air system has the associated benefits of both lower energy loads as well as reduced ductwork, both of which are cost-saving.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Exploring_Every_Possibility_31-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Exploring_Every_Possibility-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Exploratorium has multiple features designed to reduce its water consumption. Two large cisterns under the structural beams connecting the southeast pilings capture up to 338,000 US gallons (1,280 m
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       ) of rainwater and fog runoff for reuse in the facility.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The plumbing is designed for water conservation, with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waterless_urinal" title="Waterless urinal">
        waterless urinals
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dual-flush_toilet" title="Dual-flush toilet">
        dual-flush toilets
       </a>
       projected to save an annual million gallons of water.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Additionally, the bay water heating and cooling system is estimated to save two million gallons of potable water a year by eliminating the need for traditional evaporative cooling towers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ehdd.com_28-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ehdd.com-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Exploratorium at Pier 15 also makes use of natural light in the effort to reduce energy loads. The existing building had many clerestory windows and an overhead skylight that runs the length of the interior space. In compliance with historic preservation requirements, the building’s façade was left mostly unchanged, allowing for much of the interior space to benefit from the existing architecture’s ample daylight. The retrofit did include the addition of high performance glazing to the existing windows.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sustain_21-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sustain-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In January 2014, the Exploratorium was awarded
       <a href="/wiki/Leadership_in_Energy_and_Environmental_Design" title="Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design">
        LEED
       </a>
       Platinum certification.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGBC_Scorecard_38-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-USGBC_Scorecard-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to the
       <i>
        New York Times
       </i>
       , "After a two-year post-opening shakedown period of monitoring and adjusting the systems, the Exploratorium hopes to become the largest net-zero-energy-use museum in the United States and possibly the world."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hall_39-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hall-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Exploratorium is also turning its commitment to sustainability into a learning experience by showcasing elements of the building’s green design on site.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sustain_21-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sustain-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Layout">
        Layout
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Exploratorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Layout">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The new site contains over 600 exhibits, 25% of which were developed specifically for the Pier 15 site. With the exception of some art installations, all exhibits are developed and made onsite.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OpensApril10_40-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-OpensApril10-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The indoor and outdoor spaces are divided into six galleries, each highlighting a specific content group. Many exhibits are mobile, however, and move among different galleries; similarly, not all exhibits fall into distinct categories.
      </p>
      <p>
       Exhibits cover a range of subject areas, including
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_perception" title="Human perception">
        human perception
       </a>
       (such as vision, hearing, learning and cognition), the life sciences, physical phenomena (such as light, motion, electricity, waves and resonance, and magnetism), local environment (water, wind, fog, rain, sun, and other elements, as well as cityscape, landscape, and the flora and fauna of the Bay) and human behavior (such as cooperation, competition, and sharing).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MuseumGalleries_41-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MuseumGalleries-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Osher_West_Gallery:_Human_Behavior">
        Osher West Gallery: Human Behavior
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Exploratorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Osher West Gallery: Human Behavior">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blowing_Smoke.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Blowing_Smoke.jpg/300px-Blowing_Smoke.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Blowing_Smoke.jpg/450px-Blowing_Smoke.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Blowing_Smoke.jpg/600px-Blowing_Smoke.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blowing_Smoke.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Visitors collaborate to make a
         <a href="/wiki/Smoke_ring" title="Smoke ring">
          smoke ring
         </a>
         at the Exploratorium's previous location at the Palace of Fine Arts
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The West Gallery focuses on human behavior. Its signage and exhibits encourage visitors to play with perception; investigate memory, emotion, and judgment; and experiment with how people cooperate, compete, and share. It holds exhibits such as
       <i>
        Poker Face
       </i>
       (partners try to assess when someone is bluffing),
       <i>
        Trust Fountain
       </i>
       (an experimental exhibit from the museum’s
       <a href="/wiki/National_Science_Foundation" title="National Science Foundation">
        National Science Foundation
       </a>
       -funded Science of Sharing project, this two-person drinking fountain is based on the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prisoner%E2%80%99s_dilemma" title="Prisoner’s dilemma">
        Prisoner’s dilemma
       </a>
       , a classic scenario centering on negotiation and trust),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GalleriesExhibits_42-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-GalleriesExhibits-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the
       <i>
        Tactile Dome
       </i>
       , a pitch-black environment visitors explore by touch, which was originally designed by
       <a href="/wiki/August_Coppola" title="August Coppola">
        August Coppola
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coppola_43-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Coppola-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The West Gallery also included the temporary exhibition
       <i>
        The Changing Face of What is Normal: Mental Health
       </i>
       , which showcased the personal artifacts of patients from the now-decommissioned Willard Psychiatric Center,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawlessness_3-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lawlessness-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which was on view through April 2014.
      </p>
      <p>
       The West Gallery also houses the Kanbar Forum, a cabaret-style theater that will host music events, science lectures, and other programs when it opens in summer 2013.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sparkling_44-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-sparkling-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="South_Gallery:_Tinkering">
        South Gallery: Tinkering
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Exploratorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: South Gallery: Tinkering">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The South Gallery is a workshop area where visitors can engage in learning through
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maker_movement" title="Maker movement">
        hands-on making
       </a>
       , located directly across from the Exploratorium's own internal exhibit workshop, which is also open to their view. Oppenheimer wanted visitors to be able to “smell the oil”, and insisted that the usually hidden exhibit-building activities be on display as an exhibit in its own right.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shrine_45-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-shrine-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Exhibits in the South Gallery highlight a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DIY" title="DIY">
        DIY
       </a>
       aesthetic, and include
       <i>
        Animation Stations
       </i>
       where visitors can make their own
       <a href="/wiki/Stop_motion" title="Stop motion">
        stop-motion films
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Artworks on display include the
       <i>
        Tinkerer’s Clock
       </i>
       (a 22-foot-high clock constructed by artist
       <a href="/wiki/Tim_Hunkin" title="Tim Hunkin">
        Tim Hunkin
       </a>
       , with figurines in his noted cartoon style that can be manipulated by visitors and unfold into a clockface on the hour); and
       <i>
        Rolling Through The Bay
       </i>
       (a sculpture made by artist Scott Weaver over the course of 37 years, utilizing over 100,000 toothpicks and depicting many of the Bay Area’s iconic landmarks, through which a ping-pong ball can roll on one of several different "tours").
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rolling_46-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rolling-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Bechtel_Central_Gallery:_Seeing_and_Listening">
        Bechtel Central Gallery: Seeing and Listening
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Exploratorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Bechtel Central Gallery: Seeing and Listening">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giant_Mirror_at_the_Exploratorium.jpeg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1083" data-file-width="960" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Giant_Mirror_at_the_Exploratorium.jpeg/220px-Giant_Mirror_at_the_Exploratorium.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Giant_Mirror_at_the_Exploratorium.jpeg/330px-Giant_Mirror_at_the_Exploratorium.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Giant_Mirror_at_the_Exploratorium.jpeg/440px-Giant_Mirror_at_the_Exploratorium.jpeg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Giant_Mirror_at_the_Exploratorium.jpeg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A visitor investigates the reflective properties of the Giant Mirror in the Central Gallery of the Exploratorium at Pier 15.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Bechtel Central gallery houses many of the "classic" Exploratorium exhibits, including many of those that have been on display since the very earliest years of the museum.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCarthy_4-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-McCarthy-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It includes a mix of new and old exhibits that investigate physics and the perception of light, color, and sound, such as
       <i>
        Sound Bite
       </i>
       (a demonstration of hearing with the jawbone instead of the ears) and
       <i>
        Bright Black
       </i>
       (a trick of perception convinces viewers that an object is white when it is almost entirely black).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="East_Gallery:_Living_Systems">
        East Gallery: Living Systems
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Exploratorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: East Gallery: Living Systems">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The East Gallery houses a much-expanded selection of life sciences exhibits. Many exhibits relate directly to the immediate local environment, such as the
       <i>
        Glass Settling Plate
       </i>
       (barnacles and other creatures are grown on a plate in the Bay, then put live under a mobile microscope to be observed from both above and below) and the
       <i>
        Algae Chandelier
       </i>
       (visitors can pump air to nourish overhead tanks of colorful phytoplankton). Other exhibits explore different biological systems and processes, such as the imaging station with mouse stem cells, the live cow’s eye dissections, and the
       <i>
        Live Chicken Embryo
       </i>
       (one of the oldest of the Living Systems exhibits, showing live chicken embryos at different stages of development).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosenblatt_47-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rosenblatt-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Fisher_Bay_Observatory_Gallery">
        Fisher Bay Observatory Gallery
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Exploratorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Fisher Bay Observatory Gallery">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The Bay Observatory building is the only new building constructed on the Exploratorium’s campus. It holds the Seaglass restaurant on its lower level and exhibits on the upper level relating to the waterfront and the cityscape. The gallery focuses on what visitors can see in real time, including the movement of clouds and tides, the changing waterfront, the movement of ships, and interpretation of oceanographic data. The Observatory has glass walls on all four sides to facilitate observation. Many of the exhibits were developed specifically for the location, such as
       <i>
        Oculus
       </i>
       (a circular opening in the ceiling that allows the entire gallery to be used as a timepiece, tracking seasons, solstices, and the sun’s movement),
       <i>
        Visualizing the Bay
       </i>
       (a 3-D topographic map of the Bay Area that allows visitors to see real data mapped over the landscape, such as the movement of fog and the salinity of the Bay over the course of days or years), and the
       <i>
        Map Table
       </i>
       (an assortment of historic and contemporary maps and atlases displaying different views and perspectives on the landscape).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eyes_48-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-eyes-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Bay Observatory also houses the Wired Pier project, which consists of more than a dozen sensors on and around the Bay Observatory that stream real-time data about the surrounding environment, such as quality of air and bay water, weather, tides and pollution, and compile it into interactive visualizations.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plutchak_49-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Plutchak-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Outdoor_Gallery">
        Outdoor Gallery
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Exploratorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Outdoor Gallery">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fog_Bridge_at_the_Exploratorium.jpeg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="853" data-file-width="1280" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Fog_Bridge_at_the_Exploratorium.jpeg/300px-Fog_Bridge_at_the_Exploratorium.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Fog_Bridge_at_the_Exploratorium.jpeg/450px-Fog_Bridge_at_the_Exploratorium.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Fog_Bridge_at_the_Exploratorium.jpeg/600px-Fog_Bridge_at_the_Exploratorium.jpeg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fog_Bridge_at_the_Exploratorium.jpeg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Mist from Fujiko Nakaya’s Fog Bridge drifts over the water between Piers 15 and 17 at the Exploratorium.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Outdoor Gallery comprises the north, south, and east aprons of Pier 15, and extends through both ticketed and unticketed space. Focus is on direct interaction with the Bay environment, which can be seen in exhibits such as
       <i>
        Color of Water
       </i>
       (an installation of 32 distinct
       <a href="/wiki/Textile_sample" title="Textile sample">
        color swatches
       </a>
       suspended below the rail surrounding the pier so that visitors can investigate the changing colors of the Bay’s water). Another notable exhibit is
       <i>
        Remote Rains
       </i>
       , which allows visitors to choose a past rainstorm as profiled by the Hydrometeorology Testbed, which is then recreated by a rain machine that duplicates the frequency, size, and velocity of the raindrops, giving a tangible experience of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NOAA" title="NOAA">
        NOAA
       </a>
       research data on storms.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plutchak_49-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Plutchak-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huffpo_50-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-huffpo-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Along the publicly accessible bridge connecting Piers 15 and 17, artist
       <a href="/wiki/Fujiko_Nakaya" title="Fujiko Nakaya">
        Fujiko Nakaya
       </a>
       created an installation called
       <i>
        Fog Bridge #72494
       </i>
       that creates bursts of fog for six minutes every half-hour as the first in a series of large-scale temporary installations called
       <i>
        Over the Water
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-overthewater_51-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-overthewater-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <i>
        Fog Bridge
       </i>
       is 150 feet (46 m) long and makes use of 800 nozzles to create the fog, which Nakaya hopes will inspire visitors to pay attention to the nature of one of San Francisco’s best-known weather patterns. Although originally slated to be temporary, it is now on permanent display. A desalination system, located in Pier 17, conditions bay water for use in the artwork.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aleta_52-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Aleta-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miranda_53-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Miranda-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Public_space">
        Public space
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Exploratorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Public space">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The Exploratorium campus includes 1.5 acres (0.61 ha) of publicly accessible open space. This includes the plaza facing on the Embarcadero, the connector bridge between Piers 15 and 17 where
       <i>
        Fog Bridge # 72494
       </i>
       is installed, the south apron of Pier 17, and the east and south aprons of Pier 15. This public space overlaps with the Outdoor Gallery, and includes some notable exhibits, such as the
       <i>
        Aeolian Harp
       </i>
       (an expanded version of the original installation by Doug Hollis on the roof of the Exploratorium at the Palace of Fine Arts, first created in collaboration with Frank Oppenheimer in 1976)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aeolian_54-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-aeolian-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the
       <i>
        Bay Windows
       </i>
       (visitors spin disks filled with samples of Bay mud, sand, and gravel gathered from five distinct regions of the Bay itself).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baywindows_55-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-baywindows-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Restaurant_2">
        Restaurant
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Exploratorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Restaurant">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The lower level of the Bay Observatory Building houses the Seaglass Restaurant, which, like the Seismic Joint Cafe, is open to unticketed members of the public. Both the Seismic Joint and Seaglass are run by Loretta Keller, chef-owner at Coco500, in partnership with Bon Appetit Management Company.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Educational_programs">
        Educational programs
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Exploratorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Educational programs">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The Exploratorium seeks to bring hands-on inquiry to education, including training teachers in the teaching of science.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Between 1995 and 2012, an estimated 6,400 educators from 48 states and 11 countries directly participated in Exploratorium workshops.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-factsheet_57-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-factsheet-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Teacher_Institute">
        Teacher Institute
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Exploratorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Teacher Institute">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Exploratorium_teachers.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="417" data-file-width="640" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Exploratorium_teachers.jpg/300px-Exploratorium_teachers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Exploratorium_teachers.jpg/450px-Exploratorium_teachers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Exploratorium_teachers.jpg/600px-Exploratorium_teachers.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Exploratorium_teachers.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Teachers from the Exploratorium's Teacher Institute examine the "String Thing" they built
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Teacher Institute, founded in 1984, is an Exploratorium-based professional development program geared towards middle and high school science teachers. In addition to providing workshops at the museum that teach hands-on and inquiry-based teaching methods, it provides coaches and support for novice teachers. Studies have shown that while 30 to 50 percent of new teachers leave the profession within five years, the retention rate for teachers who go through the Teacher Institute is 85 to 90 percent.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olney_58-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Olney-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Teacher Institute is also home to the
       <a href="/wiki/Iron_Science_Teacher" title="Iron Science Teacher">
        Iron Science Teacher
       </a>
       , a national competition that celebrates innovation and creativity in science teaching, which originated at the Exploratorium in San Francisco. Parodying the cult Japanese TV program,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Iron_Chef" title="Iron Chef">
         Iron Chef
        </a>
       </i>
       , this competition showcases science teachers as they devise classroom activities using a particular ingredient — an everyday item such as a plastic bag, milk carton, or nail. Contestants are currently or formerly part of the Exploratorium's Teacher Institute and compete before a live audience for the title of "Iron Science Teacher". Shows are also archived on the Exploratorium's website.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IronScience_59-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IronScience-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Two out of three teachers applying were being turned away due to space limitations, by the time the Exploratorium closed at its former location; following the move to the Piers, the Exploratorium has been expanding its professional development for teachers through the Teacher Institute.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olney_58-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Olney-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As of 2013
       <sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Exploratorium&amp;action=edit">
         [update]
        </a>
       </sup>
       , two
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MOOC" title="MOOC">
        MOOC
       </a>
       courses were also being made available through MOOC provider
       <a href="/wiki/Coursera" title="Coursera">
        Coursera
       </a>
       . One course integrates engineering into middle and high school
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Science,_Technology,_Engineering,_and_Math" title="Science, Technology, Engineering, and Math">
        STEM
       </a>
       classrooms, and the other integrates
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maker_movement" title="Maker movement">
        making and tinkering activities
       </a>
       into elementary and middle school classrooms.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coursera_60-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Coursera-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Informal_learning_programs">
        Informal learning programs
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Exploratorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Informal learning programs">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The Exploratorium operates several programs centering on informal learning. The Institute for Inquiry (IFI) is a professional development program of the Exploratorium geared towards educators, scientists, administrators, and policymakers. The Institute is a recipient of National Science Foundation funding and designs programs, materials and tools to help leaders in the science education community further the role of inquiry in elementary science education and strengthen reform efforts. It consists of workshops and an online library of resources available to participants in the Institute.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IFI_61-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IFI-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Institute for Inquiry partnered with the
       <a href="/wiki/Sonoma_Valley_Unified_School_District" title="Sonoma Valley Unified School District">
        Sonoma Valley Unified School District
       </a>
       on a program combining science education with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_Language_Development" title="English Language Development">
        English Language Development
       </a>
       (ELD). Data from the two-year pilot study showed that a professional development program designed to help teachers integrate ELD strategies into science lessons had a significant, measurable impact on the achievement of students in both ELD and in science.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-innovation_62-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-innovation-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Center for Informal Learning and Schools (CILS) is a collaboration between the Exploratorium, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_California_Santa_Cruz" title="University of California Santa Cruz">
        University of California Santa Cruz
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/King%27s_College_London" title="King's College London">
        King's College London
       </a>
       . CILS studies the intersection between museums and schools as centers of informal learning with the intention of understanding how informal science learning occurs and how informal educational centers such as the Exploratorium can contribute to science education reform.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CILS_63-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-CILS-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Tinkering Studio began in 2008 as an in-house program geared towards
       <a href="/wiki/Maker_culture" title="Maker culture">
        maker culture
       </a>
       and a “think with your hands” approach. It is housed within the museum in a dedicated space in the South Gallery, where it runs free do-it-yourself activities for museum visitors; it also shares its work with a larger audience of educators in afterschool programs, schools, museums and other learning environments. It is being cited as a prototype for similar programs across the globe,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tinkering_64-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-tinkering-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       including South Korea, Canada, India, and Saudi Arabia.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mideast_65-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-mideast-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Exploratorium also operates as afterschool tinkering program in partnership with San Francisco chapters of the Boys and Girls Club. In 2012 the Exploratorium was awarded a grant to create the California Tinkering Network, in collaboration with the Community Science Workshops, Techbridge, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Discovery_Science_Center" title="Discovery Science Center">
        Discovery Science Center
       </a>
       , the California Afterschool Network, and the California STEM Learning Network. These organizations partner with over 20 local afterschool or summer programs to provide STEM-enriched activities for children in underserved communities. The initiative was designed to test an adaptable model for providing tinkering activities to promote learning and development in an afterschool setting.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cwsnetwork_66-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-cwsnetwork-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Exploratorium also houses a number of other educational resources. These include the Learning Commons, a library and media resource center that houses a collection of print and digital science teaching resources for use by regional educators; a webcast studio, located in the Central Gallery, which produces 75 educational Webcasts from the museum and locations around the world annually, including a live webcast of the Mars
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Curiosity_Rover" title="Curiosity Rover">
        Curiosity Rover
       </a>
       launch and landing; and Lifelong Learning, which creates educational programming for children, teens, family groups, and adults. Lifelong Learning programs further the Exploratorium’s stated dedication to informal learning and the museum as teaching tool, and include day camps, workshops for families, the Homeschool Science series (in-house classes geared specifically towards homeschooled students), the Girl’s Science Institute (multi-day workshops geared towards girls 9-11), and excursions for adults. The Exploratorium has also published a number of books, and many of the 50,000 pages on its website are hands-on activity ideas or science experiments in the museums’ signature open-ended style.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Educational_outreach">
        Educational outreach
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Exploratorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Educational outreach">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The Exploratorium operates several educational outreach programs. The Community Outreach Program works with community organizations to provide exhibit-based educational activities for underserved children and families in the local community.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tecolote_67-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-tecolote-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Exploratorium is also home to XTech, a science education program for underserved middle school students. Begun in 2006, XTech was primarily funded by a National Science Foundation grant and provided afterschool activities in science, engineering, and technology in partnership with two community-based organizations in the Bay Area. XTech serves over 100 students a year in addition to 10-15 youth facilitators.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-xtech_68-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-xtech-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Explainers">
        Explainers
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Exploratorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Explainers">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The Exploratorium Explainer program, which has been operating since the museum opened, hires and trains high school students and young educators each year. The program tripled its capacity, hiring 300 Explainers, following the relocation to Pier 15 in 2013.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KTVU_69-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-KTVU-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Explainers function essentially as docents. There are two types of Explainers: High School Explainers, who are teenagers, and Field Trip Explainers, who are college students and young educators. The program was conceived by Frank Oppenheimer in the early days of the museum. He wanted to provide a visitor experience that was a learning experience in a museum context
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richardson_70-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Richardson-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and allowed for guesswork and the absence of "correct" answers. He felt young people would be more capable than adults at conveying the open-ended experience he was looking for. His plan with the Explainers was to "loosen up the whole feeling of learning." Oppenheimer also intended the program to allow students to experience learning outside the framework of their school systems. The Explainers come from a highly diverse array of socioeconomic backgrounds, and he hoped they would bring families and friends who would not otherwise be likely to visit a museum.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cole_9-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cole-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         169–174
        </span>
       </sup>
       Both the High School and Field Trip Explainers are paid positions.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Explainer Program was inspired by the staff demonstrations Frank observed at the Palais de la Decouverte, although the facilitators at the Palais when Oppenheimer visited were either graduate students or practicing scientists. The success of the Exploratorium’s Explainer program led the Palais to eventually hire teenage explainers of their own.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ASTC_71-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ASTC-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Former Explainers often cite their experiences at the Exploratorium as defining elements of their success,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamlin_72-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hamlin-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       including several notable tech CEOs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verisign_73-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-verisign-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Silver_74-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Silver-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Arts">
        Arts
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Exploratorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Arts">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Walter_Kitundu.jpg">
